Definitivamente, un eurogame familiar, con una rejugabilidad que se intuye muy alta, con componente temático suficiente y para cualquier tipo de jugador.

RATING: 8
Great, great game. Can be off-putting to some people because it looks like a dudes-on-a-map wargame, but it isn't, not really. It's much simpler than it looks, as which God you win the favor of in the bidding phase STRONGLY limits what you can do in the action phase. Lots of different routes to victory, some nice catch-up mechanics, and really stunning graphical design overall.

You can tell alot of care went into the game, from putting art where it'll never even be seen during the game, to using unique minatures for the forces of each player.

RATING: 6
1 play / 5 players

LIKE: This game has a good action / auction mechanism. It tells what others are up to and it can help conceal what others are up to. The monsters are also fun to play.

DISLIKE: Wargame tension is not really my thing, but it is on the same level as Antike here, where actions are short and multiple paths to victory reduce the actual amount of attacks that happen. The dice add a big swing element. I felt ships were rather weak towards the game end.

OVERALL: It's ok and I didn't mind playing it. Reminds me of Antike, Mare Nostrum, and others, but I like this one a little more because there are quite a few combos to look for and plays quickly.

RATING: 7.9
Un mini "wargame-euro" en las Islas griegas, apoyado por una mecánica de apuestas por un dios u otro, donde el elegido dá unas acciones concretas.  Ver si aporta cosas nuevas expansiónes, pero son caras. 
Es un euro de nivel medio, casi familiar, y los componentes y el acabado del juego es precioso. Pero el juego en sí puede quedar un poco soso para algunos, que se esperen más combate u otros elementos, ya que el juego se centra más en las apuestas a los dioses y en las criaturas mitológicas.
No me gusta NADA el tema de las cartas de filósofos... al menos en lo poco que he jugado alguien ha conseguido los 8 = 2 metropolis y ha ganado fácilmente sin que de tiempo de reaccionar y te quedas con la sensación de que el resto del juego sobra y que está eso demasiado desproporcionado... para terminar pronto el juego y que no se alargue. Habría que revisar eso y arreglarlo de alguna forma haciendo que valieran más la obtención de los mismos, o pudiendo ir en contra de ellos de algún modo.

Muchos usuarios comentan jugarlo con objetivo de 3 metrópolis en lugar de 2. Que se alargaría el juego, pero que se disfrutaría más y quedaría más balanceado, con más opciones y mejor sabor.

Hay que comentar que las reglas son demasiado escuetas, y faltan muchas cosas por concretar... y es imprescindible recurrir a unas FAQs en inglés, resumen de dudas aquí en el foro BGG, que han resuelto muchas dudas que salen cuando lo juegas.

RATING: 8.91

The game has a slow pace, especially at the beginning, due to the limited movement opportunities.

Kings-making can also happen in this game.

RATING: 9.5
Cyclades is an incredible game! Simple enough to be explained but there is a ton of game in it. I wouldn't describe this game as strategical, because it's more about tacticts and adapting your moves to the new situations that come on the board every turn. I really like the auction mechanic: it may be strange for a war game, but it works perfectly and is also very deep. It's not just managing money, because it's also important to dissimulate your intention and read those of your opponents to come back on the God you want in the right moment. The components are amazing. The graphics are stunning, especially the board, and the plastic figurines for the mythological creatures and different ones for each color's army make this game simply wonderful.

The base game has some issues still: it's too static, often the winning depends on Pegasus and the game can also drag too much, if the players don't start building. Fortunately the expansions fixed these problems while also adding so much more meat. With all the modules together this game becomes an incredible light civilization/war game, that also provides a great thematic experience.

The only reason for me not to give a 10 to Cyclades with the expansions is, that the enjoyment you can get from the game still really depends on the group you're playing with. I think some people don't enjoy the game so much, because they see a war game and they start making strategies, while Cyclades requires you to adapt constantly to new situations, that can otherwise really mess up with your plans. This leaves some players with the feeling that the game is unbalanced and sometimes they complain (at least in my experience) during the game, making my enjoyment go down a little bit. Nevertheless this game can offer a really fun experience.

Rating without the expansions: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A nice and light civilization and area control game. You have to bid for the favour of the Greek gods in order to be able to build armies, ships, buildings or to gain gold. Then you try to conquer as many island as possible with different building because if you manage to control five different buildings you will be able to build a metropolis. Two metropolises get you absolute victory. There is a basic, luck based (dice) combat, and also you can recruit mythological creatures to aid you. It has a great design, easy to learn mechanics and it plays out quickly. The downside is that without the reference sheet it is hard to figure out which creature what power it has.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Cyclades is a mix of mechanics that I personally find enjoyable for a game of this type and bunch of specific issues that I really do not like at all. 

It's one of the most weirdly balanced games I've played. From the very first round of the game you are always a hair's breadth away from being completely screwed over and knocked out of the running at the whim of almost any player and the dice. The only thing holding back a pure blood blath is the fact that your attacker may accidentally screw themselves over by the self-same luck of the dice. Okay, so it's Risk. But I don't like Risk very much.

It is better than Risk. There are ways to more tactically and strategically build up defenses and/or attack strength. And interestingly, the only way to take any action of any kind is to win the appropriate auction for that action at the start of each round. So now it's an auction game with really, really difficult decisions.

The win condition is also interesting enough (build 2 super buildings) and there is an extra cool twist of recruiting mythological creatures every round if you have the cash for it. Essentially, you buy a one time special action. 

But then things get really crazy. Some of the mythological creatures are really just plain broken (either overall, or just crazy powerful if they come out in the right situations). That is, if the right special action comes out at the wrong moment for you (pure luck), you could easily be decimated by your opponents even if you were in a previously strong position! It's like throwing in an extra layer of just pure random over the strategy of the game. I'm not particularly a fan of that maneuver even though I actually do find the abilities and idea of the mythological creatures appealing in general.

So in the end Cyclades has cool mechanics and stunning production values with its board/bits. However, I don't particularly appreciate the tension that I or anyone can get nuked out of the game with hardly a moment's notice at the whim of the cards or way too Risk-like opening. Still, it's an interesting game that has a fun core and a cool theme. For now, I'll call it decent. Further play could shift that opinion, but I don't know if I'll ever really like just how fragile the game feels and how anti-climactic the end can suddenly be.

RATING: 6
Cyclades is an unusual mix of euro-ish auctions and development with simple conflict mechanics and take-thatery (through the monster cards).  In this, it reminds me somewhat of Bruno Cathala's earlier design (with Serge Laget) Senji.  While Senji strikes me as the more original design, I think that Cyclades is the better game. 

The development aspect means that the game does progress inexorably towards an end, rather than bog down in interminable leader bashing.  Though bashing there must be if a runaway leader is to be avoided. Income is of central importance; without it you will lose bids for the actions you need to win.  The Pegasus card seems overpowered; if it appears in the end game it can determine the result. Overall, I found Cyclades quite engaging to play, though perhaps lacking the depth to keep it returning to the table.  

The artwork and production are up to Matagot's usual high standards. The monsters are particularly well done.

Look at that players: we produced a king-making effect!   

It additionally incorporates one of my least favorite auction mechanics which far too often forces players to bid on things, and win things, they do NOT want because they were bumped from the one thing they did want.  Yes, you can try to strategize around this result by faking bids for things you don't want, in the hopes of getting bumped into things you do want, but really, why bother because doing all this isn't fun anyhow.  Better to play something else that you do enjoy.  Not-interested-in-playing-again.

RATING: 9
I've had this game for a while now and can't believe how long it took me to get this to the table (mostly due to my groups hesitance towards games going over an hour and me being unsure of my handle on the rules).  Now that I've finally played it... Wow.  An amazing game whose mechanics allow for a lot of interesting options to take and contemplate upon each turn.  Gameplay is very streamlined; collect money, bid for turn order/ god powers, take your action phase, restock & repeat.  One big positive I can say for this game is that there seems to be no fat in it, in that everything in the game works together to create various subtle combos for players to explore each game (especially in terms of using the mythical creatures).  A truly great game with some declicously blended mechanics.

Also: great components, player miniatures differ from one another and the rest of the components are printed on some good quality cardboard and cut in a way to further emphasize the theme.  Box could be a bit smaller, but don't let that stop you from trying/ picking this game up!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5

Also alot of turtling happens, but that is not neccesarily a negative to me. In summary the game falls between two chairs, trying to be simple and somewhat enginebuilding, and ends up being mediocre at both. Its being lauded as an euro, but it essentially feels like an ameritrash to me.

RATING: 7

These other aspects derive from the other gods' powers and thus it is possible to position yourself with multiple options to obtain victory.  You won't be able to switch your strategy mid-stream though (without ensuring a loss) and that gives the game a more linear feel.

RATING: 4
Cyclades started out as a game I'd rate a 7 or 8.  After my latest play, though, the rating has dropped dramatically.  This game can DRAG.  If a player looks like they are about to win the game, other players will cooperate to outbid him, use the mythological creatures to steal or destroy his gold/cards/figures, and usually set up another player to win.  This process continues until one player hoards so much gold that the end is inevitable or the players decide to call it quits.  

Zeus' ability to cycle through creatures over and over again is just irritating, as is the Chimera's ability to retrieve discarded cards.  That means really destructive creature cards can be used multiply times in quick succession, adding more chaos to an already chaotic game.  Planning and precise bidding matter little in the endgame, as good players will see exactly what you need to win and stop you at every turn.

RATING: 7
A BGG-s képek alapján durvább látványvilágra számítottam, de az a kiadás, amivel én játszottam, csak néhány lapkát és fabábúkat tartalmaz, ott nem voltak műanyag szörnyetegek:) A játék maga jó!! Tetszik az istenek keverése (4 fővel játszottuk, 1 mindig kimaradt), a mitológiai lények sokszínűsége, a licit viszont erősen Amun-re beütésű, majdnem ugyanaz a mechanizmus. A különbség az, hogy itt levásárolható a licitre áldozott összeg, bármennyi is legyen az. A játék egyébként nem túl komplex. Sajnos nem nagyon csatáztunk, és a lapkalerakásoknál elkövettünk egy hibát, ezért mindenképp játszanék majd még egyszer vele.
---
Másodjára 2 főset próbáltuk ki, immár (remélem) helyesen játszva mindent. Tanulságok: egyfelől nagyon jók a mitológiai kártyák, a reménytelen helyzetekben (pl. ha nem jön fel a szükséges isten, vagy esélytelenek vagyunk a liciten) általában csak ezek segíthetnek. A másik, hogy nem szabad a döntést (harcot) a végére hagyni, mert nagyon hirtelen vége tud lenni a játéknak (1 óra kb.), figyelni kell, hogy hány kör alatt tudják a többiek befejezni.
---
Kieggel is kipróbáltam, így 2 és fél óra volt egy 4 fős, de nem feltétlenül a kieg. miatt, talán csak többet gondolkoztunk.

The experience with that gamer has been washed away with our last play which came down to the wire.  It is that excitement which makes the game worthwhile and one of the more memorable times we have played a game like this.  If you like Euros but always wanted something more, buy this game.  If you like Amerithrash but thought a military game needed an extra something, buy this game.

RATING: 9
Tense, fast moving area control game with very important role selection mechanic. Very playable with 2 but I suspect will really shine with 4. Fantastic artwork and pieces - great quality package. Rules are simple and straightforward and the game plays quickly for what it is. Great medium weight game. The only mild criticism I have at this point is that it's a winner-takes-all game rather than a ranked one (using VPs) so one person will probably win by themselves and everyone else will lose, rather than coming second, third etc. Very finely balanced game though so it's unlikely you'll feel you lose due to poor game mechanics rather than good strategy. I suspect a lot of people will blame the game for not letting them take the roles they want where instead they're probably not bidding high enough during the auction phase.

RATING: 7
Cyclades production values and how it straddles between Euro and Ameritrash should be sufficient to satisfy both camps. Being a Euro gamer, I enjoy that aspect of it more, but I also find the player interaction and the table talk of trying to keeping each other in check engaging. The auction mechanic is innovative and remains fresh on subsequent plays. Cyclades doesn't overstay it's welcome; playing quickly enough to keep you satiated and hungry enough to have you come back for more. 

Bring your big boy pants though; because there's a lot of attacking and defending and feelings may get hurt.

I'm not too sure about the cycling of the gods to find the one you need. I can't place my finger on it, but there's something amiss with it. I guess I need to play the game more to confirm or dismiss my suspicions.

RATING: 10
Even though it looks like a strategic and combat-heavy game, Cyclades is neither of both: actually the most decisive battles aren’t fought by the armies on the board but by the coins on the worshipping board, to get the gods’ favour. Nonetheless Cyclades isn’t either too unbalanced towards the resources’ development: it simply offers a good blend of different playstyles, kind of a neutral ground appeasing both the strategic and the euro gamer.

I really like how the mythologic side was handled, for the game’s authors show respect both for the setting and all things classical. Moreover, the theme doesn’t feel like it was stuck on the game afterwards just because it’s trendy or looks cool: on the contrary it gives the impression that it was developed within the game, for it is well integrated into the mechanics.

Cyclades is a rounded off and well balanced game, allowing many different strategies: every game is undecided and unpredictable till the end and thus till the end every player still has good chances to win. Victory always comes unexpected.
This is the first time I rate a game with a 10.

RATING: 3.99
Xssassin said it best, and I quote

"10
[CK]

[CK#] Collective Kingmaking
(A game state where players influence their opponents' board position so pervasively that victory is achieved by the player who the group implicitly allows to win, rather than the player who makes the best decisions.)

Is nothing sacred? Let's examine Cyclades to see just how much of a player's options and infrastructure are freely available for his opponents' to disrupt... Physical territory and gourds can be conquered. Boats, buildings, and troops can be destroyed. Money, priests, and philosophers can be stolen. A desired deity can be usurped with no possibility for the player to over-bid. (This includes turn order because the mechanic is entwined with the role selection.)

It turns out the aforementioned elements comprise Cyclades in its entirety, meaning each of your opponents can deduce your best course of action and intentionally thwart you at every junction, if they so desire. Put another way, a player cannot win unless his opponents implicitly allow his victory to take place.

The alleged balancing factor here is self-preservation; it's not directly in anybody's best interest to shaft an opponent. Consider deliberately overbidding on a deity - you pay more than the deity is worth, your opponent gets an inferior action, your other two opponents rejoice because they just spent nothing and saw two of their opponents' positions worsen. (This process is effectively like casting Hymn to Tourach on the guy to your left in a four player game of Magic.) Of course, the action is necessitated if that opponent is on the precipice of victory, and the only recourse is shaft-the-leader. While something like El Grande ends after a set number of turns, Cyclades drags on until its specific victory condition is met, and the end-game (which can come very quickly) is reduced to shaft-the-leader(s) until nobody can, or is willing. If performed to its theoretical maximum, all players begin the turn with an equal shot at the two metropolis goal and whoever goes last, or gets lucky, or is allowed will win.

Thus, a game of Cyclades will span many turns, so infrastructure cards are crucial. Apollo exceeds Zeus if you can protect the gourded island, meaning the best course of action is literally Apollo Apollo (...) Apollo Apollo Athena Athena Zeus, with a stray Ares in there somewhere. In other words, stockpile gourds on an island protected by troops purchased from Ares until you can Athena twice for a metropolis and Zeus, flushing monsters to find Pegasus - or recur it - and sending your troops to a lightly defended metropolis. (Alternatively, Athena twice more.) Problem is, thanks to collective kingmaking the best strategy won't actually work because decent opponents will overbid Athena or steal your philosophers. The futility is the same for any strategy enacted by the perceived leader. Second place has never looked so appealing, and in all honestly probably never should."

However, my rating is a 7 if table talking is NOT allowed; a ONE if people feel it's OK to tell each other how much money they have so that they can screw the leader.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I was late to this game and I wish I hadn't waited. This has been an enormous hit with my game group. It is not a hybrid just for the sake of being innovative, the Euro and thematic elements work perfectly together, producing a satisfying tension throughout. It seems that it would be hard to play this game and not indulge in some engaging diplomacy, too, as the bidding is such a crucial element. The game is deep, with many interesting decisions popping up, often different kinds of decisions in different games. The game is simple to learn, the components are beautiful to look at and the game doesn't overstay its welcome. Cyclades will have its proper place next to Chaos in the Old for top shelf hybrid games though Cyclades is much more accessible mechanically and thematically.

I have heard some complain of the "smack the leader" trope being too dominant in this game. There's only one thing worse than this, of course, and that is having no opportunity to interact to get in the way of a player pulling ahead of everyone. This second problem thankfully is not an issue with Cyclades.

Those seeking to expand the game's thematic options, increase direct conflict and mod the game in varied ways will absolutely want to check out the excellent Hades expansion.

Update: The Titans expansion (not desirably compatible with Hades) fires the Hades expansion. Titans is the expansion Hades should have been. I was actually planning on moving Cyclades down to an eight for me but Titans wakes it back up to a full nine.

RATING: 7
Es un juego de civilización en el que comienzas con presencia en un par de islas y tu objetivo es conseguir dos metropolis, que puedes obtener de varias maneras (construyendo, "comprandolas" o arrebatándoselas a otro jugador). En cada turno el jugador debe encomendarse a los dioses.. según el dios escogido podrá realizar unas acciones u otras. La elección del Dios se realiza por subastas... también hay unas cartas de "criaturas mitolócias" que se pueden comprar y añaden un factor estratégico al juego.

Es sencillo de jugar y los turnos son muy rápidos, pero aunque no hay muchas cosas que hacer (se usan pocas fichas durante el juego), hay mucha estrategia en cada decisión.. y tienes que tener en cuenta qué pueden hacer tus oponentes a la vez que te centras en tu propio objetivo. 

Es entretenido. Lo probé a cuatro, pero probablemente el juego se muestre en todo su esplendor jugado a cinco jugadores (así no hay ningun dios oculto y todas las acciones posibles están disponibles para la subasta).

RATING: 6
Looked forward to playing this fairly straightforward area control game with nice theme and bits. Finished with a bit of disappointment that mythical creature cards seemed to unbalance play too much. Tentative assessment and rating. UPDATE: This has become an instant hit with my high school and college age boys. The lighter game play with the theme is similar to Small World in its appeal as a fun strategy game. I still feel the creature cards are a bit chaotic, but that lends to the appeal for them, as well as being thematic. The ability to choose actions in any order promotes a flexible, tactical flavor to the game. It is also too easy to access by either appearing on the board and being purchased cheaply, or being fished out by the Chimera card from the discard pile for a cheap victory. UPDATE: Take out the Pegasus card, or create a house rule (either: only 1 unit can be "flown" or moved with Pegasus, like the Harpy, or it can only be used on a deserted island). Otherwise, game seems to be broken. An 8 with the house rule.

RATING: 8

RATING: 6
I thought this was clever in a lot of ways, and it seems like a game I should like more than I did. But there is some serious broken-ness with the mythic creatures cards in the endgame, with a discard-retrieval card and Zeus' ability to endlessly cycle until he finds a card he needs being particularly problematic. Like so many Asmodeé games I've played, it's frustrating close to being quite good, but has a crippling flaw.

Others have commented on the pacing issues, and I think that's a legitimate gripe. It takes a long time to get anything going in this game, as you can do only one thing per turn and doing anything meaningful requires stringing together 3 or 4 different actions, with intermediate results not being that rewarding. The Mythical Creatures might provide an interesting outlet for mitigating this somewhat, but as I say they've got some issues.

Anyway, it's not a bad game, but this is a genre of games (king-of-the-hill wargames) in which it is notoriously difficult to do anything good, and I'm still sticking with Star Wars Risk or Conquest of Paradise I think. Those games give me more turn-to-turn satisfaction and sense of drama.

RATING: 7
Based on 1 play with 4 players:

Our first and only game came down to kingmaking, making it all feel incredibly anticlimactic. Not sure how common this is or if it was because we were all just n00bs. A pretty big bummer for a first game though.

The auction mechanic is cool and effective. You win and lose by your plays here. ALL of the fun and tension in the game came from trying to bid JUST enough to deter other people from ousting you. Loved it!

The only real problem I had with the game is that, like many other games in the Euro-Risk genre, it's better to just play defensively than attack anyone like... ever. You make yourself far too vulnerable if you strike first, so combat ends up feeling useless and boring. If combat had been a bigger part of the game my rating would be a LOT higher.

Art and theme are obviously rad. EXCEPT SIRENS IN GREEK MYTHOLOGY WERE BIRD WOMEN (YES, DISTINCT FROM HARPIES) NOT MERMAIDS. GAWD.

RATING: 9.5
Only played with two players so far and waiting to rate until played with 5 players. I think this game will be best played with 5 players. 

Played it with 4 players and it is so far my favorite game. Very little luck involved and a lot of strategic choices from the start of the game. It can be a bit too much for new players and it take a couple of games to truly appreciate it, but when you do it's wonderful. The best part is that the games finishes when someone has reached its goal and does not suddenly stops and everyone counts their points. This adds a lot of excitement to the game and it can be really tense. 

The artwork of this game is truly exceptional and the figurines are super detailed with each color having different soldiers and boats. For such a complex game it's not too long clocking in at 2 hours playing time which is just right. 

The game is really balanced so it's the better player that will win. If you like chess but would like a theme I would recommend this game. 

The only negative I can say about this game is that it's a bit complex to introduce to new players especially if they're not used to a bit more advanced board games and the price. But when you open the box you will understand why you pay a bit more for this game.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Cyclades mixes elements of euro games and ameritrash games, and even though I'm not a fan of the latter, I think this game is quite good - it's not a favorite of mine, but it's a very different game than so many others. In my first game (4p with my gf, her father and my brother), the 3 guys made a lot of offerings to the war gods while my gf slowly got the favor of the philosophers etc and actually managed to get her 2 metropolises quite fast even though we tried to deny them to her. So I guess the game is a bit more of a euro game, but it's still refreshing to play a game like this from time to time.

Still, I think there are some issues with the game. It takes a while to play, and the core mechanic is basically the auction for the gods (which, admittedly, can be quite intriguing, especially when you get outbid and make a low bid on another god because you hope to get outbid on this god too so you can go back to the first god)! 
I'm not a huge fan of how the combat system works. I think building up your forces and attacking takes too long. On the one hand, this is good as your opponents can plan how to defend themselves and perhaps win the next bid on Ares so you cannot move your troops. On the other hand I think it makes the game less fun as there aren't that much combat in a game as a player needs at least two turns to recruit and move armies through his ships. This means the game becomes about planning sequences of moves across several rounds while at the same time managing your economy so you can win the necessary bids to carry out your attacks. 

The game isn't all about attacks, actually on the contrary; it IS a eurogame with a main mechanic centered about auctions. You can even win by not attacking at all (I've seen it happen, though it rarely does).

The mythological creatures add a nice variety to the game, though the symbols can be quite hard to understand, so you'll constantly need to look them up in the rule book. This is a small minus in my book, though it can be mitigated with repeated plays. I especially like Pegasus as it can be used to make a surprise attack - I actally won a game once by using it on a guy who had built his second Metropolis that round and thought he had won, but I captured it and won instead, which was quite an epic ending.

However, normally the ending is a bit anticlimatic to me because suddenly someone has won because they often just builds the last building they need for their metropolis (or just bought Pegasus and swooped in and claimed a metropolis!), and nobody can react or do anything about it. It's not the game's fault, I just prefer games where you tally up your points in the end. As this is one of the few games without VPs, obviously you cannot do that.

Oh, and last thing: having played with the expansion, Hades, I must say that I will suggest to include that expansion most of the times - it just adds so many cool features and makes it a more brutal game with much more interaction and interesting decisions (however, the Hades module also seems a bit clunky).

Rated 8 after one play. Well, first play, and I personally really enjoyed it, but overall our group of four probably had a more overall sobered reaction. Update: ...and that's what happened. Eventually traded this away for Ora et Labora.

RATING: 8
Cyclades was one of my first "big box" games with a ton of components. It's an interesting mix of building up armies, capturing territory, maneuvering across the small islands, getting into bidding wars, and so on. The individual mechanics fit together very well, and victories are not always certain. It always seems like everyone is one step away from winning near the end of the game. And unlike many other wargames, it's difficult to turtle up and bide time: everyone is immediately a target because of how close you start to each other, and how temping the islands and prosperity spaces are to capture.

It also needs to be mentioned that this is an absolutely gorgeous game. From the box art, to the player screens, to the cards - it's all very visually appealing.

I'm getting close to wanting to pick up the Titans expansion. Co-op in particular sounds really fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
One play at Spiel 2009, but totally botched due to a rules explanation error which seriously affected combat. I think I want to purchase the game then—despite some reports of rich-get-richer problems. I attribute these to not valuing the offending cards and chits correctly. A priest should be hideously expensive simply because he is rather powerful... In any case, the RRP is very high, and that might stay my hand for now.

[b]Update[/b] I was able to purchase a copy in the recent months (at the time of writing), and confirmed that the game is much better with the correct combat rule. At the same time I think the actual game is weak, making me regret my purchase to a significant extent. The reason is that with less than the full 5 players, one god is semi-randomly withdrawn from every round; and that combat has been split over two different gods. This means it can take quite some time before the first fights break out: fighting is costly and thus requires preparation, even if it just to make sure you get your hands on the god you need. Now the game has been balanced thus that the other two gods are dangerous in their own right, so technically that makes up for it. But it does nothing to alleviate the game's slow pace which has players shifting tactics all the time.

While annoying, what actually nags at me far more is the very partial reactive nature of Cyclades' game play. Players are not busy planning their way to victory; instead they are pushed onwards by the every growing influx of money and special cards and buildings to strike out quasi-randomly against the others, hoping that this buys them sufficient time to worm their way to victory. If I see someone gaining more money, I want to do something about that [i]right away[/i]—but no, I may not be sufficiently close, the gods may not be available, another player may consider the situation not yet sufficiently dangerous and block me for his own ends, ... and by the time I arrive at the scene another bushfire has sprung up demanding my attention elsewhere. As a result, undesired and/or unrecognised situations may persist for too long for the players to do anything about them anymore. So I make do and hope it is sufficient. Usually it isn't: In all of the games I've played to date, I've been relentlessly reduced from a competitor to a figurant doing nothing but stalling other players at the expense of my own position, and ultimately just being either out-money'd or out-war'd. If I look at the strategy guides and reviews and responses of fans to complaints, they always speak of actions as if sufficient financial resources are available. 'Purchase threatening creatures before someone else can', 'Bid up highly for Athena because of the threat of multiple philosophers', 'Use the Kraken!', that sort of thing. Yes, of course, obviously. If I could I'd definitely do so. You will also see that a good creature just [i]happened[/i] to arrive at the right moment. Yay for yourself. But what happens if you [i]don't[/i] have the resources to do the above, and that the creature stack is shitty and uninteresting? Well, then the answer is, from my experience, that you're losing the game and are really just delaying the inevitable. Cyclades doesn't really allow the players to claw back from a weak position; cooperation between weak players is hard as the game treats them as natural competitors just the same; and whoever ends up in a weak position is due to unpredictable (not necessarily random) effects to begin with. All you can do is react to other players, exploit their weak moments, and hope that you come out of the initial struggles with the best papers.

I should add that the above doesn't mean that you will always win; you will simply be a strong contender for the win as the competition has been seriously reduced in both power and number. I find myself caring less and less who eventually wins in this case, or even the game itself: my beef is with the fact that the road towards that finish is far too often unfair and game-technically uninteresting.

I keep open the option that with more experienced players matters become more exciting, but for now I'm not as enamoured with the game patterns in this game as I thought I'd be. Part is unwarranted expectations, but part is also disappointment with what [i]is[/i] in the game. I'm putting in a rather lower rating than I wanted the rate to be.

[b]Update[/b] As part of an Alphabet Challenge to play through my collection from A to Z this game was of course also on the roster. But in over a year I never managed to round up sufficient enthusiasm to play this title with 4 or 5 people. I drew my conclusions from this, and let the game go to a place where it will be looked after better.

RATING: 8
Tight gameplay that tasks players with finding a delicate balance between offense and defense. As with most games using this auction mechanic (e.g. Evo), making players overpay for a desired effect is a strong move, as is keeping prices level to prevent players from building an economical advantage. Battles can become rather swingy when mythical creatures are involved, creating new opportunities every turn.

RATING: 7
Interesting cross between an auction game and a wargame.  In Cyclades, players attempt to build two metropolises, which can be acquired culturally (by building the right buildings or bringing on philosophers), or by force (from other players).

To gain all these things, players need to increase their revenue, and then make offerings (bids) to the gods - Zeus, Ares, Athena, and Poseidon.  While Ares and Poseidon will build your armies up (to attack or defend), Zeus and Athena provide cultural benefits and special discounts.  Alternatively they can honor Apollo and reap smaller economic benefits.

I like that this game isn't too much in either genre to turn off the average player.  But it felt a bit dry and that you didn't have the space to really create an engine to make powerful moves - perhaps it's balanced to a fault.  Also I find that the need to "shuffle" the large god tiles is ridiculous; I ended up using an option in the Start Player app to determine the random order each round.  Overall this is a slickly produced game, but I don't think it will be a favorite for our group.  I'm curious to try the expansion.

RATING: 6
Components: Stunning artwork all around. The board, god, and creature art are all magical. The miniatures are super cool, especially the fact that each player's pieces are different.

Experience: Unfortunately, the theme just doesn't go with the game mechanics. Half of this game is a tight economic game, with bidding, earning income, investing in tech, and buying improvements. The other half is a war game, with in-your-face aggression that is out of my comfort zone. Neither of these really fit with the fantasy theme I was hoping for. Feels like Hellas on steroids.

With so much conquering going on, it seems pointless to even invest in my own buildings when someone else can just come take them.

The creature cards add some cool effects, but not enough to really make it fun. There is a lot of chaos.

I'd like to play again, but hopefully with more players so I can see the board and auction in all its glory, and hopefully turtle into some corner of the map.

Last Played: 2011-11-01
Eagerness to Play Again: Within a year

Plays (1):
3 players: 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A wild, swingy, and constantly entertaining game of tense auctions, dudes-on-a-map combat, and area control. Simple to learn and play but very hard to play well. Because a player can lose a metropolis or an entire army in a single combat, the current winner can never get comfortable and the current loser can never be counted out. If you're in the mood for a game that is predictable, merits long-term planning, and is low on conflict, this is not for you. Otherwise, you're going to have a blast.

The only real complaint I have is that Zeus' cycling ability leads to too much end-game digging. As a result, we always house-rule that every subsequent cycle costs one more coin than the last and that seems to solve the problem well.

Also, damn is it a gorgeous game.

RATING: 5
Civ growth/conflict game where you can only move your armies or ships, etc if you've won the auction. This does create some interesting tactical options since only one player will be moving armies, or ships, etc.
OTOH, it also makes for a wierd slow motion growth where winning the right auction at the right time becomes very important.
Add in some chaotic action cards & monsters and some lumpy plastic and you've got something which smacks a bit of a 70s combat game, but has some euro auction action.
I can take it or leave it.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
My favorite game. I hesitated to give it a 10 for a long time because 2p can be brutal if the players aren't evenly matched, but that's blaming the game for a fault in the players. By that logic it would be impossible for an area control/direct conflict game to reach a 10 since that's the nature of the beast. Having realized this, the game is ideal: perfect 10.

2013: On subsequent plays, it also scales very, very well.

Can be taught in minutes, endless possibilities and true freedom on your turn to do whatever you like in any order. Makes you wonder why so many games have so many rules.

Cathala avoids the pitfalls so many designers fall into: hex map instead of diagonal moves on a grid, adjusted values on d6 so battle is flavored by luck, not solely determined by it, and a simple ruleset that can be explained in minutes.

RATING: 8.25
The game mechanics engage the theme very well - the bidding for buying of the favour of the Gods, is derivative ('Evo', 'Amun Re') but is very well integrated and spiced up nicely by randomising the turn order. The combat system is simple but has enough nuance and variability to be exciting. There are 3 paths to securing the victory and the game keeps the finish pretty tight resulting in tense games in which everyone is usually still interested for the duration.

It has a reasonable playing time and some pretty good art and is excellent component wise.

An 8 after 2 games but likely to edge up.

RATING: 7
A game of bidding with conflict that is like a knife-fight.  Players start at close quarters and the ability to initiate warfare is dangerous and risky.  There is subtle jockeying for position, but every play toward aggression is completely telegraphed.  The tight range between game start and victory conditions makes this dance even more tense as the game quickly acquires the feeling that someone could win at any moment.

Has been underwhelming for my group, but I am bullish on the design.  I feel there is a great game lurking just beyond our limited experience.

RATING: 7
A game where everyone is jockeying to be the first to gain a certain amount of resources.  Towards the end, the game everyone nears the finish line and players take turns preventing others from winning.  Finally, when enough players are close the finish line, it can no longer be prevented and a couple of players cross.  Tie breaker goes to most money.  I see this game tie'ing often.  Normally I would rank a game like this a '6'.  But this one gets an extra point because of the fun mechanic of choosing deities (amen-re bidding) and the many monsters + figuring out how to best use them to get your resources (while thwarting others).  Not sure I'd want to play it that often, but fun enough.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players try to be the first to own two metropolises in the Greek Isles.  Fun, fast playing civilization growth game, where players bid in Amun-Re style for the right to use a god this turn.  Zeus is all about priests and temples, Ares about troops and forts, Poseidon about fleets and ports, and Athena about learning and universities.  You only get to do one "thing" a turn, depending on your god, so it takes a while to get anywhere, but the turns are so blindingly fast, it doesn't appear to be a problem.  Metropolises are claimed by cashing in a set of four buildings, a set of four philosophers, or by taking it off another player via Ares.  On top of that, mythic creatures toss a bit of chaos into mix.  Easy to teach and play - a nice 60 minute game with a bit of grunt.  The usual Matagot stunning bits too.  Well done.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
So far I've only tried it once with 2 players, but it was fun, and seemed to have a fair bit of depth.  There were a few rules hiccups, and the rulebook could have been laid out better and contained more comprehensive information, but we got through it with the help of a posted FAQ.  I think it is a game that will be better with more people, so I'm looking forward to trying that.  Rating could go up or down based on further plays.

Update: After further plays, I find the endgame comes up to quickly and then everyone tries to figure out how to stop someone from winning. It also seems like the kind of game where everyone needs to know how to play otherwise it unbalances the game too much. Not ideal for the type of play groups I usually have.  Putting it up for trade.

RATING: 9
A true hybrid boardgame, with part clever euro bidding - very reminiscent of Amun re, and a dudes on a map style game. Being a hybrid you'd think it would get played more, however it tends to be played less, as generally caving in to player count or other players whims, rather than a utilitarian approach to game selection it doesn't quite satisfy either camp. And often feels that something is missing. The ending can be abrupt, which is ameliorated to a large degree by the titans Expansion which makes the game more combat orientated, and takes the player count up to six with team play. 

However, though this game doesn't get lots of play, every six months or so I feel like playing it and find it extremely satisfying at these times The game play is solid, the components lovely and not over produced, I've had this game for maybe six years and I still feel affection for it, it's a great design and though not my favourite I still see myself enjoying it for years to come. 

I see it as being quite different to Kemet, which is a more intense experience, has better components and is much more of a war game, but I also prefer cyclades on all counts.

RATING: 7.5
[b]Tipo:[/b] Competitivo // [b]Interacción:[/b] Media // [b]Azar:[/b] Bajo // [b]Rejugabilidad:[/b] Media
[b]Cartas:[/b] 52 de 58x88 mm
[b]Fundas recomendadas:[/b] Ultra Pro (tamaño "Standard american")



[b]OPINIÓN PERSONAL:[/b]
El Cyclades es un juego bastante sólido que se adapta punto por punto a mis gustos: tiene bastante interacción entre jugadores, relativamente poco azar (prima la estrategia), partidas de duración media (unos 90 min), un tema y una estética atrayentes... desde luego, si buscas un juego de conquistas con gestión de recursos (el oro) y construcción de edificios/flotas/tropas, el Cyclades puede ser una buena opción.

De hecho, si no le he puesto más nota es porque tiene un par de inconvenientes que (para mi gusto) le impiden ser un juego de sobresaliente:

 -> Las veces que he jugado, me ha dado la sensación de que después de hora y media de desarrollo más o menos pausado, la partida termina repentinamente. No es uno de esos juegos donde se va viendo quién se desarrolla más y al final de la partida gana quien ha sabido controlar la situación a lo largo de la misma; aquí lo que hay es bastante "tortugueo", con los jugadores reuniendo fuerzas en su rincón del tablero para intentar conseguir repentinamente la victoria en un único y decisivo movimiento (gana quien se hace con dos metrópolis, así que no es tan extraño que alguien pueda conseguir ambas en un solo turno si dispone de la fuerza necesaria para ello).

 -> A causa de lo anterior, la interacción con los demás jugadores es bastante menor de lo que se podría esperar en un juego de conquistas. Todo el mundo teme enzarzarse demasiado pronto con otro jugador, pues eso podría significar quedar demasiado debilitado y permitir que un tercero se alzase con la victoria.

A pesar de estos dos puntos, se trata de un juego muy recomendable, y tiene un par de mecánicas muy bien pensadas (especial mención a la fase en la que los jugadores pujan por ganarse el favor de cada dios).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
When I was a kid, my favorite game was "By Jove," a sort-of Monopoly clone where you fought monsters, completed quests, and bargained with the gods, all with a Greek/Roman mythological theme. Since the game was dictated almost entirely by die rolls, it's tough to enjoy it as an adult.

This is why Cyclades is among my favorite games. It takes my beloved theme of Greek myth and mashes it with Euro and war game elements into something tense and meaty with strategy.

RATING: 7
Players fight for control of Greek islands, making sacrifices to the Greek gods in order to get access to their powers -- Ares to recruit and move soldiers, Poseidon to build and move ships, and so forth.  Each god can only be used by one player (the highest bidder) each round, and there are also mythological creatures which can be summoned to attack or interfere with opponents.   First player to build (or conquer) two metropolises wins.

RATING: 9
This game looks like a wargame, and acts like a wargame, but the strategy is really in the bidding phase.  As such, it was a much different game the second time we played than the first.  The first was more about positioning and maneuvers.  We laughed at that 10+ spot on the bidding track - who would bid that much?  The second game was all about saving up the gold to grab the crucial god and/or creatures at just the right time to strike the deathblow, and we had bids around 20 gold.  The tension was delicious.

This game is very good, and is one that will really shine with repeated play.

RATING: 9
Cyclades takes troop deployment, movement, and combat; building construction, resource gathering, card selection, and boils all those things down into one major decision each round:  which god do I want to bid on?  There's so much to do in this game, and I really love how the auctions on the god track keeps players focused.  That element of the gods dictating what you can do makes the complexity of the game really manageable and easy to digest. 

This game is also about ten months pregnant with tension as it's usually pretty easy for everyone to get a few steps away from winning.  

I also really love how you need to double bluff your way to victory.  By that, I mean you need one obvious and one not so obvious win option on the board that your opponents will delightfully and mercilessly screw you out of.  They'll feel so triumphant because they bound you, caged you, and threw you into the depths of the ocean, absolutely destroying every chance of victory you ever had.

But then comes the "Aha suckers!" moment where you break your bindings, escape the cage, break through the surface of the water, and micturate all over every one who thought you were so thoroughly beaten; because what they failed to see was the mostly hidden third option that relies on a convoluted four card monster chain.  I always feel like the winner of this game performs the best slight of hand.   Eighty percent of the game is setting up this mind blowing magic trick that will leave everyone groaning in defeat.  It's like having David Copperfield on your living room table.  It's not about auctions, ship battles, infantry skirmishes, building buildings, or getting money.  It's about magic.  Everything else is illusory.

One of the coolest games around.  Unfortunately, the peeps I play games with don't share my love for it.  Perhaps it's because I'm always calling them suckers.  

I have played this game a few times with players who kept doing Appollo, hoarding money, and never using it.  It can be really disruptive to the balance of the game if that money isn't being put to use as using money is how you keep other players in check. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
This is a Euro/AT hybrid.  It looked to be an excellent game due to the amazing artwork and how the board scales based on the number of players.  Sadly, the game didn't live up to expectation due to the fact that any given player may only attack on land once per round and on sea once per round (barring special mythological creature cards).  Not only that, if a player does get to attack twice in a round, that means no other players can attack!  What kind of multi-player conflict game have rules set up so that you may not even be allowed to attack in a given round.  Yes, I understand you can win without taking part in many conflicts but............ blahhhhhh!!  It's kind of ridiculous to me.  There were other issues that I didn't like but the above is the crux of it.  There are better Euro/AT hybrids available.

RATING: 6
Cyclades is a rather amalgamated euro/at game. Constant bidding to do what you want to do, limited turn actions, and dice-resolved combat. There's a nice looking board and some equally nice sculpts. A revisit to this title has made me appreciate the game a lot more. I still am by no means a fan, but I won't be throwing down the veto when this comes out again.

RATING: 7
What an interesting hybrid game. I should love this game as it has a euro auction element I love in other games (Amun Re, Vegas Showdown) as well as the tactical considerations of a wargame. But my reaction is one of fatigue when I think of this game because of the heavy negotiation and constant leader bashing and rubber-banding required. Like Cosmic Encounter, alliances need to constantly shift with constant accusations of "He's winning! GET HIM!" The constant finger pointing wears me out.

But my problem with king-of-the-hill type games is that they tend to drag on and on as players collar the one about to win, only to then set up one of them as the new leader who must be collared which makes a new player the leader who must be collared…

RATING: 7
Cyclades is a euro-war game that captures the price of waging war. Players compete to build or capture cities from each other. The driving force to the game is the auction round. To win, players need to bid carefully to preserve their limited bank but also push up the cost of auctions so that nothing is too cheap.

RATING: 7

RATING: 8
Positives 
easy to teach.
easy to play. 
Auction mechanic is simple and fun.
No run away leaders.
60-120 mins.
gorgeous.

Negatives
table talk (however, more often then not this works to the advantage of the quite players).
unbalanced Zeus ability (which is fixed in hades).
broken Pegasus ability.

Summary

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Highly recommended.

Labelled as a hybrid, but to my mind, firmly a euro. The combat is rarely that swingy and it's the play around the battles that is the meat of the game. The auctions are a big chunk of the game and they're a lot of fun. As a result the game is really a game of planning. Very rarely can things be done in a single turn and you need to carefully and efficiently build up to your decisive actions. Players really need to be vigilant about what other players are up to. There can be a slightly unsatisfying finale to the game if you don't keep track of the less exciting routes to victory - philosophers and buildings.

The board and all of the components are gorgeous - excellent miniatures; nice irregular shaped coins. It all looks fantastic on the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Highest Recommendation.

- minden játékos megnyerheti gyakorlatilag bármelyik pillanatban, így kicsit azon múlik, hogy ki ellen mekkora energiákkal tudnak a többiek összefogni, megelőzve ezzel a játék befejezését.

RATING: 7.5
Asmodee is consistently putting out well designed games with gorgeous production and Cyclades is no exception.  The auctions actually make thematic sense (unlike the vast majority of Euro games) and the combat is simple but effective.  Each player color has different molds (nice touch).  The game is fast paced, so every decision matters.  There appear to be multiple ways to achieve the same end goals, so much more to explore in this game.  One of my favorite 2010 releases

Includes: Hades and Titans expansions

RATING: 4
A game of bidding on gods and placing troops to control territory. Since what you can actually do on any given turn is very limited, the game feels stunted and slow. Board position is almost completely static.

Still, the game staggers along until the end game, where it completely grounds to an agonizing halt. One or two players will be about to win, and everyone must gang up on them to prevent them from winning. Not fun for the players getting ganged up on. One or two players will have no chance of winning, but will have to fall on their swords to keep the game going. Not fun for those players. Eventually (this part took over an hour in our game), someone will sneak through and win.

Our game stretched to 2.5-3 hours, and at that length the game is absolutely awful. 90 minutes of it was interesting, but the end game was miserable.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Amazing components and theme. This game reminds me of the Dune board game in the way that you bid for powers and how battles are resolved. On the plus side, it's much much faster. (1-2 hours I'd say.)
Players can gang up on other players, which sucks when it happens (to me :()...but the victory conditions and map are always so tight that the end-game is pretty tense.
Nearly everyone I've played with winds up with that feeling, "with just 1 more turn I could have won!"

I do have a problem with allowing as many mythological creature cards to be played as a player can afford. Once someone develops a strong income engine, the last few turns get ridiculous.

RATING: 6.5
My initial rating was based on one play at Essen. This was one of a handful of games that were on my "must play" list (Matagot plus Bruno C = probably a winner!) so I was really looking forward to it. I know that, like another commenter, we received incorrect rules instructions (which we spotted after a few turns), but I don't think that play was affected too much by that. My main disappointment was the "snowball effect" built into the game - anyone getting even slightly ahead in treasure tokens or priests can effectively bully their way into getting whichever god's favours they want, simply by virtue of being able to outbid other players at no cost to themselves. 

Update: I played the game again with a better rules explanation and having read some of the comments here. Although I still think the snowball effect is present (and could dominate), it was a much closer and more enjoyable game - we understood the use of the monsters better, for example (even though few were used). I even managed to very nearly sneak in a win - being forced to almost constantly choose Athena can work to your advantage if nobody's keeping an eye on you!

As a result, I have increased my rating.

RATING: 9
Excellent hybrid between american and euro design which plays very smoothly. Cyclades is a dudes on a map where the main way to select your actions is through winning the right to do them via intense auctions. The production is great, with vibrant art and well made miniatures (with different molds for each player color!). 
Teaching it is a breeze, mostly because it looks more complex than it actually is.
It also plays quickly, compared to most other games in its type.
Bonus points for playing well with two players!

RATING: 7
A nail-biting bidding game where sometimes you bid on a thing you don't even want in the hope that somebody will outbid you and leave you free to pursue your actual plan.

But what you're bidding on is actions in a dudes-on-a-map game, and despite the Euro mechanics + tension, often I really just don't want to build armies to defend buildings. So for me, the strategies are cleverly balanced in terms of their game-winning potential, but not in terms of how much fun they are. And I think that not feeling fully flexible keeps me from sinking my teeth all the way into this game.

RATING: 8.5
A very good game where the available actions are auctioned among the players every round. There is a single objective - to have two metropolises - but several ways to accomplish it. There are quite a few random elements to spice things up, and mythological creatures have a very important role in helping or disrupting the players' best plans. In fact, the luck of the draw that dictates the  order of their appearance may be a bit excessive sometimes. The game may also drag a little as the players try to prevent others from winning. But it has just the right level of confrontation to fit the theme without making it lose the Euro feel. There isn't a single novel element, but known mechanics are combined into a unique and very interesting game. On the production side, it is as gorgeous as I have come to expect from Matagot, with wonderful miniatures and Miguel Coimbra's artwork.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Cyclades may look like a typical "dudes-on-a-map" game in the vein of Risk, but in practice this is only about half true. The other half of the game is an auction, yet these two sides are beautifully intertwined into a cohesive whole.

The goal of the game is to control two metropolises. A metropolis is comprised of four different buildings. Each building is granted by a different god, so a player wishing to construct one will have to eventually win the favor of all four gods. However, one can also control a metropolis simply by taking it from another player. The board contains various islands, some of which generate money each turn. This money is what is used to bid on the favor of the various gods. Yet each god has a different way of bringing you closer to your goal. Some allow you to move your people around the map, gaining more money production or fighting over buildings/metropolises, some make things cheaper for you to buy, and some can even help you towards a metropolis through card collection (a completely different way of getting one). 

The real brilliance of the game comes in forcing tough decisions on a player. There is never enough money to do exactly what you want, and players will step on each other's toes even without direct attacks. There will be times where you need to go first in a round, but this means you will go last in the next round. Masterful play demands planning AT LEAST a round ahead, sometimes more. Each god allows for various abilities, all useful, but all demanding payment. And that's not even getting into the various mythological monsters that show up each round. Some are extremely powerful, and a player that ignores them (or at least their potential) will be in a world of hurt.

Cyclades is conflict done right. It is a brutal struggle against the other players at the table that manages to avoid feeling like a "take that!" kind of game. Every action you take has far-reaching effects on everyone else at the table, and they know it. Their question is the same question you must answer about them: is it worth me paying to stop this from happening or should I focus on my own goals? Every turn is a choice between defense and offense, or some hybridization. There is never a "surprise win" or runaway leader with experienced players. It always comes down to management of risk and management of resources. This game is a masterpiece. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Not a bad Euro-American-style hybrid. I do think the Euro parts work better though. It has a nice auction similar to Amun-Re where if a player outbids you you're forced to bid on something else. Every player ends up having bought the special powers of a specific Greek god, which allows for different special actions. Meanwhile there are about 2 dozen special power cards that you can buy for extra cash, ranging from useless to absurdly powerful. I didn't like this bit as much, especially because they really require a separate sheet to decipher--the hieroglyphs on them are laughably obtuse. The random combat is so-so. I tend to play defensively in games like this, buy enough guys to keep others from messing with my plans with their random combat. It almost worked, too. The presentation goes pretty far: the minis and the board are very nice, as are the graphics, barring the low usability of the iconography. If this were an ugly game it would get a 6, but it's a pretty game, so 7. (rating after 1 play)

RATING: 7
If I said that Cyclades was a Mediterranean based civilization game where each city state attempts to build up their nation with fleets, armies, cities and philosophers, comparisons to Gerdt's Antike would be accurate enough, but...

In some ways Cyclades feels stready & inevitable, like Antike, but it adds the interaction of auctions for turn order, the creatures to increase intercation and special activities, and a small slate of available actions for each God in a player's turn.

The result is terrific.  It's a shoulder to shoulder rush to take and hold onto enough resources to climb to the top of a slippery rock, but taking care not to let someone else follow you, or find an even better path to the top.

Strong game, justifiably top 100 stuff. 7 on an initial solo play w 4 players

RATING: 8.5
Great multiplayer conflict game because of:
- well implemented theme
- the auction mechanic (same as in Amun Re) makes the choice what to do much easier
- simple and clear set of rules (combat and maneuver are very simple but yet effective)
- variety through the mythological creatures
- it is not chaotic (as in everyone attacking everyone every turn)
- lot of playing experience in a rather stort play time

The downsides for some people (but not me) might be:
- dice have a say in combat results
- it is a conflict game, i.e. you have to attack and you will be attacked

RATING: 9
Played first time with boys (9 & 11). All of us enjoyed it. Younger son even won after invading his father's isle and taking over his metropolis. Will have to teach him a lesson next time we play. :devil: Most likely he will just school me again. 

We all liked the the minis and the mix of Euro and Ameritrash. Beautiful board and bits. Would be cool to add metal coins and get minis painted. 
Slow build up to the end with the choice of two gods each cycle (3-player game). [Update: We forgot to shuffle the gods after every other cycle. Would have made it more interesting.] Would have liked to have a choice of all four, but see how that could wonk it all up a bit, especially the bidding, which I enjoyed. 

Definitely going to be played again soon. I'm sure wife/mom will like this one too.

A few days later and I enjoyed my revenge vicariously as the wife invaded the younger son's island to capture his temple and port for the win! And a new family favourite was born! 

It's nice to see the boys progressing beyond children's games and easier family fare. We're not giving anything up, just expanding horizons, and Cyclades is a great first "gamer's" game for the whole family. 

The rule set is a bit extensive but not extreme. It's pretty straightforward and makes the turns pretty simple. You just have to plan ahead, save up some gold and hope the right gods turn up when you need them.

RATING: 8
Very interesting game!  You control armies and fleets in the aegean and maneuver to meet the victory condition (two metropolises).  Each turn you bid on one of the gods, each of which is associated with a unique order (such as Mars for moving armies and attacking with them, as well as recruiting more armies and also buying one of the ingredients for a metropolis, a fort).  It's actually a fairly simple game of positioning and bluffing.  You can normally see an attack coming a turn ahead and head it off.  The most interesting aspect of the game are the monsters, each of which has a special power, which you can hire at the end of each turn.  Typically someone wins by making a surprise move by hiring a monster and using its power in a way no one had foreseen.  Very fun when you pull it off!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
The game ends when a player gets two metropolises, which can be a somewhat lengthy process if you build from scratch.  Much quicker however, if you conquer them!  I suppose some people may not like having the ability to swoop out of nowhere to win by taking over a metropolis, but that's part of the tension in this game, and spurs much of the strategy discussions on the table.

RATING: 8.5
Really beautiful game and I'm not talking about the components. The design is perfected in such a way that allows one to have a deep and fun game in a short amount of time (relative to other games of this type). In addition, the game can be taught in less than ten minutes or so.

The only down side for newcomers are the individual creatures cards that come up but, since each new card starts at the "4" spot, as long as you pause to explain what each one does when it shows up it isn't a problem. Of course, they might ignore the significance of a particular card (or combination of cards) but that is what experience is for and building that in Cyclades is a pleasure.

This game is tight, it begins with a relatively constricted position - it gets right to the point - dilly-dalliers *will* lose. This doesn't mean you need to attack everyone, in fact, with Athena and a peaceful use of Ares every now and then can win the game.

One of the flaws of this game becomes evident when you play with sufferers of analysis paralysis: It's difficult to convince them that sometimes it's impossible to predict what will happen when there are three or more people behind them to bid one of three or more auctions. You can just see their eyes shifting to each god and their gears turning... "Well, if I bid on Ares for 6 and John picks Poseidon and Mary then outbids him and John is then forced to try and take Ares from me and if he does and I then choose Athena for 3, will Mike really let me have it or will my underbid force him to outbid me so I can bid on Ares again... but what if Mary outbids me instead of kicking John off of Poseidon... ??" My last game, which was a blast, dragged on because of one person going through that process turn after turn. Some things just aren't predictable but just try and convince an AP sufferer of that...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I might raise my rating with additional plays. Probably the best dudes on a map game I've played. I don't generally like dudes on a map games, though.

RATING: 10
- Love this game because, despite all the back and forth, it often comes down to multiple players all being able to win individually in the last round.
- I like how it's possible, but not necessary, to play aggressive right from the start. Many games it's very difficult to successfully pull off an early push for the win. Players can also win through turtling, a surprise push, or some card+action combos.
- Despite the previous comment, the game doesn't really suffer from a run-away leader problem as it's not impossible for players to come-back after a massive beat-down, or take down a player with a strong position.
- Definitely recommend the expansions. Titans effectively turns this into two distinctly different games. I can't make my mind up as to which I prefer, however there are expansion modules I enjoy with/without Titans.
- Teaching isn't too hard. 
- 5-6 players, can be a little slow, though still fun.
- Bit of randomness, but manageable and everyone's in the same boat.

RATING: 10
Cyclades hits a sweet spot for me. Great interplay of engaging theme and mechanics; fun, meaningful and competitive player interaction; memorable sessions through emergent narrative; excellent balance of skill and luck, with just the right amount of randomness to keep players on their toes at all times.

It's not a perfect game (but then, no game is). Five-player games in particular can involve a late-game kingmaking pile-on and drag on for too long as a result. Sometimes the randomness goes a bit over the top and no amount of skill can help you win when the circumstances simply favor another player too much (mostly through the monster track and battle dice pratfalls).

But none of that really matters. No other game has ever made me feel such joy as my best Cyclades games have, regardless of whether I've won or lost.

Cyclades is currently the best game I own and one of only two games I've ever played that I can confidently rate a full ten.

RATING: 9
Cyclades is a game of beautifully controlled chaos. There are enough stable elements that players can form and implement a robust strategy, but enough random elements that those plans are continually subject to fate and chance. The auction mechanics provide layers of decision making and tension. The variable order of the powers has a tremendous impact on the game, and is a factor all players must navigate. The mythological creatures bring an arsenal of possibilities to the fray, and this addition alone takes the game from good to great. These factors make for a dynamic, engaging experience as well as immense repeat playability. Add in the high quality components and stunning artwork and Cyclades earns masterpiece status.

RATING: 7
Cyclades es un juego de conquistar/defender territorios. Los jugadores lucharán por hacerse con islas donde puedan construir cuatro tipos distintos de edificios. Cuando un jugador consigue los cuatro automáticamente se transforman en una "megapolis". El objetivo del juego es tener 2 de ellas al final de una ronda.

Hay dos grandes mecánicas en este juego. Una de ellas es la fase de subasta, donde los jugadores pueden pujar por conseguir el favor de 5 Dioses distintos. A través de estos Dioses los jugadores pueden reclutar tropas, construir barcos, coger sacerdotes y filósofos y también construir el edificio especial de cada Dios.

La otra gran mecánica es el uso de las cartas mitológicas. Se colocan en un track y cada ronda es rellenado hasta tener 3 cartas, pagando 4, 3 y 2 monedas por usarlas, dependiendo del sitio en el que estén. Estas cartas cartas aportan una manera distinta de conquistar o defender islas.

Pero la parte más chula de este juego es su grafismo... es impresionante, desde el tablero hasta el libro de reglas.

SOLTAD AL KRAKEN!!!
[color=green]
LAST PLAY: 2011
[/color][color=blue]
Cyclades is a game about conquering/defending territories. Players will fight for taking islands where they can build four different kind of buildings. When a player gets the four of them automatically they turn into a "megapolis". The goal of the game is possesing 2 of them at the end of a round.

There are two big mechanics in this game. One of them is the auction phase, where players can bid for getting the favour of 5 different Gods. Via these Gods players can recruit troops, build ships, take priests and philosophers and also build the special building of each God.

The other big mechanic is the use of the mythological cards. They are put in a track and each round it's filled up to three, paying 4, 3 and 2 coins for using them depending of the place they are. These cards add a different way to conquer or defend islands.

But the coolest part of this game is the artwork... it's awesome, from its board to the rulebook.

RELEASE THE KRAKEN!!!
[/color]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5

RATING: 6.5
Lovely looking game with a neat bidding mechanism to determine who can do what.  In the end, however, Cyclades is still a typical light multiplayer wargame with all of the usual problems - the player who avoids combat best will have the best chance, all of the players need to constantly keep each other in check and the game will end when someone makes an error which inadvertently hands the game to another player and the best strategy is to 'fly under the radar' and look for an opportunistic win.  Other games which have exactly the same issues are: Mare Nostrum, Antike, Twilight Imperium.  All of these games have various bells and whistles which make them look different but they all boil down to the same kinds of situations - the question is how fun the ride is to get to that point and how drawn out the endgame is.  Cyclades is a fun game, but don't come here expecting new solutions to the same old problems.  I expect that eurogamers will find the direct combat annoying and ameritrashers will find the bidding annoying.  I think the euro side of the game is the stronger.  I DO like the auction for the gods abilities - it works very well - but the whole wargame side of it is just a bit pop-gun: there is no teeth. I just don't FEEL like I am in a clash of the titans type event - I think that is inherent in such a game which reduces a wargame to its bare basics and sacrifices epic-ness for speed of play. Its a simple trade-off which everyone will have their own point on the spectrum. Imagine a spectrum with Twilight Imperium III at one end and Cyclades at the other - I would sit at about half way at a two hour game with some thematic depth: Mare Nostrum and AOE3 would be there. I used to think Shogun was there too but its actually longer than those other two with less thematic depth (its still a very good game). That's not to say that these games are perfect - they aren't - but they are for me the right balance between theme and playing time. But I can also play TI3 and Cyclades too.  Other random thoughts: Pegasus is just too powerful compared to the other myth creatures.

RATING: 5
I have a moderate dislike for conquest games, mainly since I dislike the "gang up on the leader" combat aspect that goes along with them.  Cyclades definitely addresses it somewhat, since it requires a bit of work (including winning an auction) to be able to attack.  But I found that the auctions, which I anticipated being one of favorite parts of the game, ended up constraining your choices in a way that I found too limiting.

I can appreciate what the designers were trying to do - I think that Cyclades gives a fairly good civ-lite experience in a good time frame.  That said, I'm tired of these types of themes and have a bias against these types of games anyway, so my rating is not entirely fair.  I wouldn't be against trying the game again, but I can't see this one ever becoming a favorite.

RATING: 7.5
Interesting game based on Greek Mythology. Part of game is bidding (players bid gold to the Gods to get their powers), and the other part of the game is war. Players attempt to knock the other guy off their islands. I suppose its possible to win without fighting (i.e. all Priests, but if the other players catch you doing it, you'll be pretty defenseless).

Problems: the biggest one is that victory is visible (you will know when someone is going to win). If a player is one step away from winning, the group will have stop him. So there is too much kingmaking near the end.

Components are great, its quite fun and the game is simple enough to learn for newbies.

RATING: 6
Cyclades is a Euro-Risk hybrid with dudes on a map and set collection to obtain game-winning buildings, ideally when no one can simply yank them away. Euro-like elements include auctions for roles (which comprise sets of specific actions and purchases), engine aspects (income and discounts); and tactical inertia (creating lanes of attack and attacking normally can't happen in a single turn). Risk-like elements include dice-based combat resolution (albeit with a compressed range of variable outcomes); action cards that shake up game fundamentals (notably by increasing or decreasing tactical inertia); and targeting other players with hostilities (which may involve transfer of hard-earned resources). My concern is that the game will tend to approach an endgame where everyone has one metropolis built, someone will acquire a second, and one or two other players will be in a position to relieve them of it, possibly through overt collaboration. This can happen in lots of map-dude games, but here the game end conditions - and the steps taken to snatch the victory from one player and hand it to another - are especially transparent.
