update : watch the other players very close.  My wife tricked me so easily tonight spreading around buildings and collecting philosophers.  I concentrated on a sea entrapment strategy but she thwarted my efforts with a fierce sea attack.

5 player review, 2.5 hours: That was extremely entertaining.  It came down to the wire after a decisive battle with the leader to everyone being on the edge of winning.  Maneuvers were made and at least 3 players were on the verge of winning.  Who won?  The non-gamer at the table who had quietly amassed fortune and troops to snatch victory with a huge bid for Athena, got the needed philosophers and built her 2nd metropolis.

All in all a fun game with moderate strategy and much leader bashing.  Dice rolls were cheered on.  Most entertaining was positioning one player to take out the leader, only to realize we were setting him up for the win.  It led to massive infighting amongst the losers.  Really fun to watch happen.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Cyclades is, to me, Risk combined with Worker Placement on a sparser map. The transformation is magnificent. Care has been made to ensure that every path is a path to victory. Athena can net you a Metropolis, conquest can new you someone else's without as much time or work ... but requires the proper use of two gods and thus at least as many turns, Zeus gives you a marvelous discount that gets you ahead in the bidding war and Apollo gives you a leg up in bidding through sheer cash--gives your wallet a 1-turn break, increases your income, and gives you a little gold up-front.

It's brilliant. The map itself, too, is a masterwork. The land/sea divide makes it an intensely logistical game and makes war-making a robust and rewarding process.

Cyclades is a game of bluster, of misdirection, and of count-downs. You're always watching the count-down until your opponent has roughly too much money, is too close to that second metropolis through too many means, has too many troops, has too much naval control. You're watching the imaginary count-downs until your opponents get what they want (or take what you want) and the experience is intelligent, thrilling, and lovely to look at.

One cannot talk about Cyclades without mentioning that it is an utterly gorgeous game. It is one of the prettiest objects I own, certainly among the most lovely board games next to Letters from Whitechapple and Cyclades's own companion game Kemet.

RATING: 9.5
Me and my girlfriend really love this game.

It's easy to teach and to learn, but it's really deep too. You almost always have a lot of option each turn. Each god gives you interesting options and none is a bad choice. Also, if you get stuck with a god you didn't need for this turn, you can alway buy a creature to screw your opponents (except if you got Apollo). Played this game a lot of times and it never feels repetitive since the gods available and their order is random eahc turn, and the creatures available also. 

Most wins are always a surprise. You have one player well on his way to win and bang! On the last turn, it's the least succesful player that manages a masterful stroke to win. For example, in one 2 player game I had no metropolis and my girlfriend had 2 (you need 3 to win in a 2 player game). In the last turn, she managed to build her last metropolis for an almost certain win, but playing last, I managed to gain a fourth philosopher so I got my first metropolis, i bought a University to complete my set of for building which got me my second metropolis, and I bought the pegasus card which allowed me to invade one of her island and steal one of her metropolis which got me my third. You should have seen the look on her face...

The game is built in a way that you will never be stuck with nothing to do on a turn. You can obtain a metropolis through contruction, philosophy or military might. 

You could break the game by abusing the power of Zeus, cycling through the creatures deck and coming up with unbeatable creatures combo, but it is easily house-ruled.

The other thing that might be problematic is that you have to build a bridge of ship to be able to invade with your troops. It works fine in the game, but it is strange and most people struggle with this aspect of the game.

My favorite game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Cyclades is a quick game with lots of direct conflict. On first glance, I had a few preconceived notions about the game that colored my experience. I was screwed over early in the game, pouted for a bit, complained about how the game might be "broken," but then managed to mount a comeback. In other words, I was a sore loser and a whiner for no good reason. I didn't win, but the game was better than I thought it was going to be, and I immediately wanted to try again. Lesson learned. I have to watch myself when I lose -- how much does the bias of losing affect how I feel about any game?

Luckily, that was years ago and I've had the opportunity to play Cyclades again. The game is certainly fun. It's a quick-paced conquest game that asks the players to balance action selection, defense, and offense. The mythological creatures add spice to the game; however, they can also be very powerful and lead to large swings of position (you may like or hate this element of the game).

I have a new concern now, however, and that is one of king-making. Towards the end of the game, someone may need to prevent another player from winning. If he or she doesn't prevent the win (perhaps because of oversight or because he assumed someone else would), subsequent players may have no way to do the same. In this case, the first player will win in a king-maker scenario. This is very similar to multi-player [thing=8051]Attika[/thing], for example, and it can be dissatisfying.

The game is solid, but certainly not one of my favorites. I'd happily play it again if someone put it on the table, but I think I'd aim for a four- or five-player game so as to increase the chances of everyone having to battle someone else tooth and nail -- which should in turn cut down on king-making opportunities.

RATING: 7
A curious fusion of Euro mechanics (bidding for abilities/actions, buildings that grant bonus abilities before being turned into bigger buildings to win, etc.) and pure Ameri-trash (army and monster sculpts, die-rolling battles). Works well, but Cyclades has some weird dynamics, where at some points the rich seem to get richer (players who secure/grow their income sources are able to win bids and get the actions they want, which leads to more income, armies, buildings, etc.), while at other points the leader-bashing is pretty intense (depending on what monsters are available). As a light war game, Cyclades works fine, but it's chaotic and the winner will usually be not the person who planned and played best, but the player who didn't get picked on the most. Not my cup of tea, but it is an interesting little hybrid game.

RATING: 10
Just a fantastic game that uses all of its mechanics so well! The bidding is tense and the area control is great. It is not as combat oriented as Kemet, and with the different ways to get you metropoli (strong economy, philosophers, battle) there are a lot of valid strategies. The thing that I love most about this game, and I feel the same way about Kemet, is that you have no idea who is going to win until it's over. It feels like everyone is just a small push away from winning it all. Plus with the hidden coins, you never know who can make the bigger push. One of my favorite games.

If you like games like Caylus, but you do not want the high level of its complexity, and you like games like Mare Nostrum but you do not want to play for 3+ hours, then you will love Cyclades!

RATING: 6
Played with both 2 and 4 players. Cyclades combines different mechanics (conquest, auction and action selection) into a very confrontational, action-packed experience. With three ways to obtain your cities and the huge amount of player interaction, you need to be on your toes the whole time. You also need to keep a close eye on the possibilities that the mythologic creatures offer each turn. Victory is always nearer than you think. Still, there seem to be opportunities to catch up with the leader until the very end of the game. All in all, a very interesting and original game with lots of theme for those who do not mind a lot of confrontation. For me personally, it is a bit too fast and confrontational. The artwork (the art on the cards as well as the sculptures of the minis) is great, although the materials (cardstock and plastic) could have been a lot better.

RATING: 8.5
Cyclades is amazing for giving you that epic wargame feel in under 2 hours.  Albeit slower paced than some, and more focused on the auction mechanism than combat.

It can make you impatient as it takes several rounds to achieve something as simple as sending troops to another Island, but that's also sort of it's charm and realism.  You see your opponents move fleets on a turn and you know their objective giving you a round to prepare.  I say realistic because it would have taken years to train troops and sail fleets out.

As slow paced as the game is, the ending can sometimes come out of nowhere once the end game Metropolis start popping up.

Cyclades: Titans really elevates the game to an exciting, more confrontational experience with lots of fun additions.

Base game: 8
w/ Titans: 9

RATING: 8
The next game was 'Cyclades' which I feel is best described as a Greek myths and legends mini-wargame although victory stems from building a minimum of two coliseums in the basic game.  

Each player bids to win the favours of the gods in order to obtain boons from Apollo, Ares, Athena, Poseidon or Zeus towards the ultimate goal above. Players must generate income from their initial islands plus any subsequent ones plus any gained from cards during play.

Players who are outbid must replace their icon on another god which can be one that has not been sacrificed to or overbid on another one until only one player has a bid against one god. There is an element of bluff and counter bluff in this mechanic which I like.

Once the bidding process is complete, in descending order of the god tile placement (reshuffled each turn), players take actions in accordance with the favours that the god grants. Apollo grants gold and cornucopia. Ares grants soldiers. Athena grants philosophers. Poseidon grants ships. Zeus grants priests.

Soldiers wage war and capture islands. Philosophers can contribute towards building a coliseum if four are owned by one player. Ships transport soldiers and secure trading lanes. Priests reduce the cost of sacrifices when pleading with the gods.

Mythical creatures can be bought, in god sequence order, and assuming sufficient gold that can be crucial to a strategy if used correctly. This will take practice as remembering all of the powers by picture recognition can be difficult! Watch out for the kraken, the gryphon and the chimerea!

RATING: 6
I think the sweet spot for this game is 4 players. Cyclades is really a clever Euro game dressed in Ameritrash clothes. I like the simplicity of the board, units and combat. The focus rather is on the auctions, which become very interesting because every round at most one player can recruit/move armies, one player can recruit/move boats, etc., so the game becomes about planning sequences of moves across several rounds and planning your economy so you can win the necessary bids to carry out your attacks/builds. The creature cards are just the right amount of chrome to shake things up and keep the game dynamic without over-complicating things. All in all a clever title I'm looking forward to playing with some regularity.. the painted miniatures of my friend's copy don't hurt either...

RATING: N/A
In this latest collaboration between Bruno Cathala and Ludovic Maublanc, players must buy the favor of the gods in their race to be the first player to build two cities in the Ancient Greek island group known as the Cyclades.
\13\\13\
Victory requires respect for all the gods - players cannot afford to sacrifice to only one god, but must pay homage to each of five gods in turn. Each turn, the players bid for the favors of the gods, as only one player can have the favor of each god per turn - and each player is also limited to the favor of a single god per turn.
\13\\13\
* Ares allows the movement of player armies and the building of Fortresses.
\13\* Poseidon allows players to move their navies and build Ports.
\13\* Zeus allows his followers to hire priests and build temples.
\13\* Athena provides her worshipers with philosophers and universities.
\13\* Apollo increases the income of his worshippers.

RATING: 7
A stunningly beautiful game that's just dripping with theme. The game presents a mythological Greek war, which is fought by winning auctions for the favor of five different gods. It is a strange combination of Euro abstraction and Ameritrash warmongering, that not only works; it's a lot of fun. Production values are through the roof, with the armies of each different color having their own unique sculpt and the summoned monsters that can remain in play also getting their own awesome miniature. I've only got to play it once so far, so this is a first impression rating. It could conceivably go up a point or two if it stays interesting through multiple plays, but if it doesn't I doubt I'd drop it more than a point. It played well out of the gate and It's simply too creative of a design for me to consider it a bad game.

RATING: 9
[i]Rating with all the expansions added to the base game (right now I give the base game a solid 8 - it used to be a 9, but going back to the vanilla game recently made me realize that the expansions have really elevated this design to a whole new level).[/i]

First of all, this game looks stunning! The graphic design and components are a real pleasure to look at. Mechanics-wise it is a well-balanced mix of elements taken from euro and ameritrash games. The gameplay is full of player interaction. I love games that make you remember the most brilliant moves that you were able to pull off - well, this is one of those games. 

It's not a flawless game by any means (the ending is often anticlimactic), but I always have a blast playing it.

[b]/update (September 19, 2017):[/b] Once you add all the expansions (Titans, Hades and Monuments) Cyclades becomes a whole different beast. It's just fanstastic, I can't get enough of it. I used to think that the vanilla base game was amazing, but when I played it recently it felt a bit too static. The expansions add so much GREAT content that I can't see myself playing without them. 

[i]I own the following expansions: Hades, Titans, Monuments.[/i]

RATING: 9
This one's really exciting, because it has so many good aspects.  The Greek Mythological theme is tightly integrated into the gameplay.  Games can turn around quickly, where one minute someone can seemingly be in control, but then the other players can play to block any further advancement, or even steal the current advantage.  Because a player's turn actions can be performed in any order, there are often numerous possibilities in a single turn.  It is also very unpredictable, because of the random order of the Gods' appearances, and the random order of the Mythological creature cards.  Games typically end suddenly and without warning, adding to the excitement - and "masterstroke" moves are definitely likely.  The dice add some amount of luck, but not a lot since they are weighted.  The game also scales nicely from 2-5 with the adjustable board.  Overall, a really well-designed game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
By design, players are painfully limited in what they can accomplish each turn.  Often, your "action" is nothing more than obtaining a piddly extra coin (which you may or may not want) or a slick purple or white card (which you also may or may not want).

If you're lucky and your mythological signs are perfectly aligned, you may get to capitalize on last turn's fleet action to pull off the land invasion that you've planning for the past five turns.  But if not, not.

For those down with euro-trash, Cyclades is cheerfully diverting as long as the game plays in the advertised timeframe: 60 minutes or so.  At 90 minutes, I'd rather phone a stranger and chat about heart disease.  At 2 hours, I'd rather experiment with arsenic.

RATING: 8.3
Good fun! The auction mechanic leads to great competition and interaction. There is a great deal of planning, yet at the same time the game can change quickly. Nothing is certain to pan out with your strategy.  What you do at the start is interesting - do you bid for Ares or Poseidon, or go for Zeus deductions for later bidding, or for Apollo and more money. I wish Athena had more or an influence rather than philosophers and buildings - albeit she did bring several metropolises into the game. T I love the mythology in the game and the creature cards. I like the map. I look forward to reading the rule in Hades about auctioning islands.

Rules could be a little clearer (or have a index). The first run through required multiple trips to the rule book to clear issues up. You want to test something - go to my group - they can find where the problems are! 

It is hardly Ameritrash. I kind of wish there was more dice and the armies were bigger like in the original Shogun. It would be cool to then take the battles over to a tactical battle board like Titan. A cross of this with Shogun and Titan would be a super cool game. Still, I like it so much that I am going to buy the expansions.

One criticism was that people ganged up on the leader when it looked like he would win. I have seen criticism of Hades but I am hoping the power of Hades will give that leader the chance to secure his victory.

The game is a light strategy/war/euro game. A very good introductory game for war gamers. Not too brain-burny. Must accept and like diplomacy and direct confrontation though. Not for passive-aggressive/co-op type gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Played one 3-player game and one 5-player game. Traded away for Shogun. 

Like: The auction mechanism is brilliantly designed and balances the game's available actions very well. The "bumping" effect which prevents a player from bidding immediately again on the same god makes strategizing for the auction very rewarding. 

The components and artwork are fantastic. The board and god tiles are beautiful and I love the miniatures. Each player's color of armies and boats has its own individual model, which is a nice touch. 

Playing the mythological creature cards is a lot of fun. The effects of each are quite interesting, and it's great that you can purchase and use the creatures even with a god that doesn't let you move your troops or ships like Athena or Zeus. 

Dislike: Cyclades is really all about the auction and economy. Player conflict is minimal and mostly unrewarding. All aspects of the game seem designed to encourage turtling. There is the ridiculous "one island" rule which prevents the conquest of a player's last island. This seems intended to prevent player elimination, but instead has the effect of ensuring that reducing a player to one island is a bad idea, since this makes the player completely invulnerable while still having a great economy due to the Apollo one island bonus (plus the accumulated bonuses from prosperity markers). Players also can't move ships and troops in the same turn, since these actions correspond to different gods. Thus (with the exception of powers provided by two of the mythological creatures) any attack comes with at least one turn's worth of warning. All of the buildings have purely defensive effects at most, with the exception of the temples. 

The mythological creatures are wildly imbalanced in relation to one another. Pegasus and the ship-moving creature are incredibly powerful, as is the Kraken, while many of the other creatures have relatively modest effects. This wouldn't be so much of a problem if Zeus did not have the ability to cycle through the creature deck once you have accumulated enough money. This leads to players saving all of their money for one big Zeus turn for the win, which can make the middle game fairly unexciting. Also it's sad that (again with the exception of the Kraken) the beautiful creature miniatures only appear on the board for at most one cycle before disappearing, leading me to question the point of even having the miniatures (especially since the actual effects of these creatures are often muted as well). 

I am not a fan of the victory condition of having two metropolises. I like having some sort of point system to assess second place, third place, etc. It also turns the last couple turns into a series of frantic episodes of everyone else at the table reducing the leader from two metropolises to one until someone arrives at a robust winning position. I don't like this style of conclusion to my games.

Overall: I really like the artwork, miniatures, and auction mechanism in Cyclades but everything else about the game is disappointing for me. I would play again if given the opportunity, but see no need to own the game.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Theme/Art:
You can't go wrong with ancient Greece, the game looks amazing and the mechanics fit the theme (gaining the favour of gods and summoning mythical creatures).

Production quality:
Amazing monster miniatures and I love that every colour has different armies and ships. The quality of the armies and ships is not the best though, but it is good enough.

Replayability:
There are many ways to victory and the board changes with the number of players.

Depth/Difficulty:
It's not very hard to learn, it takes about 20 minutes to teach. However, there's a lot to consider each turn.
I weight it 3/5.

Strategy:
Since you only get the favour of one god per turn you have to plan ahead. You can't build ships and attack at the same time.

Luck:
Although you have to plan ahead a lot of things, the right creature can pop up at random anytime and change the game completely.

Cyclades takes 60-150 minutes to play, depending on the number of players and their familiarity with the game.

All in all a great game and I'm looking forward to buy and play it with expansions.

RATING: 1
[CK]

[CK#] Collective Kingmaking
(A game state where players influence their opponents' board position so pervasively that victory is achieved by the player who the group implicitly allows to win, rather than the player who makes the best decisions.)

Is nothing sacred? Let's examine Cyclades to see just how much of a player's options and infrastructure are freely available for his opponents' to disrupt... Physical territory and gourds can be conquered. Boats, buildings, and troops can be destroyed. Money, priests, and philosophers can be stolen. A desired deity can be usurped with no possibility for the player to over-bid. (This includes turn order because the mechanic is entwined with the role selection.)

It turns out the aforementioned elements comprise Cyclades in its entirety, meaning each of your opponents can deduce your best course of action and intentionally thwart you at every junction, if they so desire. Put another way, a player cannot win unless his opponents implicitly allow his victory to take place.

The alleged balancing factor here is self-preservation; it's not directly in anybody's best interest to shaft an opponent. Consider deliberately overbidding on a deity - you pay more than the deity is worth, your opponent gets an inferior action, your other two opponents rejoice because they just spent nothing and saw two of their opponents' positions worsen. (This process is effectively like casting Hymn to Tourach on the guy to your left in a four player game of Magic.) Of course, the action is necessitated if that opponent is on the precipice of victory, and the only recourse is shaft-the-leader. While something like El Grande ends after a set number of turns, Cyclades drags on until its specific victory condition is met, and the end-game (which can come very quickly) is reduced to shaft-the-leader(s) until nobody can, or is willing. If performed to its theoretical maximum, all players begin the turn with an equal shot at the two metropolis goal and whoever goes last, or gets lucky, or is allowed will win.

Thus, a game of Cyclades will span many turns, so infrastructure cards are crucial. Apollo exceeds Zeus if you can protect the gourded island, meaning the best course of action is literally Apollo Apollo (...) Apollo Apollo Athena Athena Zeus, with a stray Ares in there somewhere. In other words, stockpile gourds on an island protected by troops purchased from Ares until you can Athena twice for a metropolis and Zeus, flushing monsters to find Pegasus - or recur it - and sending your troops to a lightly defended metropolis. (Alternatively, Athena twice more.) Problem is, thanks to collective kingmaking the best strategy won't actually work because decent opponents will overbid Athena or steal your philosophers. The futility is the same for any strategy enacted by the perceived leader. Second place has never looked so appealing, and in all honestly probably never should.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]THE PERSEUS:[/b]

1. Fantastic mapboard. Depicts the archipelago as spied, through encircling clouds, from Mount Olympus.
2. Brilliant evocative art throughout.
3. Straightforward rules (6 pages).
4. Scales well between 2-5 players.
5. Many interesting decisions each cycle.
6. Great Clash of the Titans theme.
7. Excellent plastic miniatures - each civilisation has its own moulds!
8. Plays fast: approx 90 mins for 3 players.
9. You get to deploy the Kraken, Medusa, Cyclops, and many other mythical beasts.
10. Brutal combat.
11. Sturdy box with useful divides.
12. Multiple paths to victory.
13. Oodles of room for outthinking your friends, especially during the offering phase (bidding).

[b]THE MEDUSA:[/b]

1. Very cutthroat gameplay. If your friends don't enjoy seeing their treasure vaults raided for gold by enchanted monsters, and then that very same gold used to buy an army, which is then forced to march on their newly built Metropolis and steal it from under their noses - then open up a Euro instead.
2. Plays like a diet version of Mare Nostrum.
3. Similar, but superior to, Hellas.
4. Board is in 3 sections - which works for scaling the number of participants.
5. Offering to the gods is just a new spin on Puerto Rico's role selection, but a good spin, nethertheless.
6. Very abstract civilisation building.
7. Kingmaking is the often referred to fault of Cyclades, but that is what you could accuse most conquest games of doing. This game is no different. Yes, the rich sometimes get richer, but I believe that effective use of Apollo can mitigate this. Or, hey, plan ahead and don't invite my sword-slashing army to attack your weak, undefended island.
8. Easily mispronounced. I believe you should say: Sick-la-deez. Geekmail me if I'm wrong, please.

[url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/513144/yugblads-quick-fire-review-of-cyclades-]Click here.[/url]

RATING: 7
A great game for three or four people. The god-auction mechanic really forces players to have a backup strategy to enact when they are outbid to their favourite deity.

There are a number of ways to win in Cyclades, and unless you are paying very close attention, someone will inevitably sneak into one of them; usually the one that the other players are ignoring. This game definitely rewards going against the grain with oblique strategies.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The mechanics of this game come first...
auction/bidding: works to perfection, and the competition is fierce!
worker placement: do you build this? build that? place soldiers? place boats for travel? place future financial success? every decision has to be made strategically. It's similar to worker placement, but not identical.
combat: very simple but it isn't an integral part of the game. you can focus on combat, and you'll resort to building forces plus some luck. 
X factor: monsters from Greek mythology make the intrigue in this game high. So many choices!

you can pretty much like any game mechanic and then find yourself at home with this game. The theme is amazing, the artwork is second to none and after the first time you learn it, the visual cues all over the game will lead you to a simple understanding and brisk pace.

RATING: 8.5
Nice game. The bidding is cool and tactical and the meat of the game. The combat on the map lacks a bit in depth though. There are monstercards that make each game different enough each time and they add spice to the game. Verdict: I find it fun to play. 

It's definitely more of a bidding game then a conquering game. A very nice looking bidding game! 

There is an exploit in the base game were you can cycle the deck for a certain card. (solved by the expansion)

RATING: 7
Some of the best artwork I've seen in a board game coupled with a fun and playful design. The theme is well implemented, though the game system doesn't feel particularly 'epic' but rather like numerous skirmishes over some tiny islands. (Not that this detracts from the game.) The mythological creatures and their effects are what puts the finishing touch on the game for me.
